14. International Day for the Eradication of Poverty (debate)
Video of the speeches
Â 

Â Â PriekÅ¡sÄdÄtÄjs. â NÄkamais darba kÄrtÄ«bas punkts ir debates par Komisijas paziÅojumu par starptautiskÄs nabadzÄ«bas izskauÅ¡anas dienu (2025/2921(RSP)).

VÄlos informÄt deputÄtus, ka Å¡ajÄs debatÄs ir paredzÄta tikai viena politisko grupu pÄrstÄvju uzstÄÅ¡anÄs kÄrta. TÄpÄc nebÅ«s brÄ«vÄ mikrofona procedÅ«ras un netiks pieÅemtas zilÄs kartÄ«tes.

Â Â Roxana MÃ®nzatu, Executive Vice-President of the Commission. â Mr President, as the honourable Members of Parliament set into their activities, I would just like to say a deep thank you to you, Mr President, for mentioning Erasmus and paying tribute to Sofia Corradi, whose vision inspired Erasmus. We must keep that dream alive for all our young Europeans.

Honourable Members, just days after the International Day for the Eradication of Poverty, we gather here today to say that the time to act is now. Today, more than 93 million people in Europe â that is one out of five â are still at risk of poverty and at risk of social exclusion. And these are not just numbers. They are families who struggle to pay their bills, they are children growing up without access to the services that they need to develop their full potential.

Yes, we have made some progress. Today, we can account some 2.5 million fewer people at risk of poverty compared to 2019, and this is a welcome trend. But the rhythm, the pace, is too slow. The persistence of child poverty is particularly alarming. And it comes at a huge cost to our society. Childhood socio-economic disadvantage translates badly later in the adult labour market and health outcomes. Moreover, one in ten workers in the European Union â approximately 20 million people â are still at risk of poverty, a situation of great concern as well that forces us to think and to act. Failing to make the fight against poverty a true priority for Europe is simply not justifiable.

This Commission is committed to stepping up our efforts and building a new momentum. We are, as you know, preparing the first European anti-poverty strategy. Our goal is clear: not just reaching our 2030 target, the Porto target, which is about taking 15 million European people out of poverty, out of social exclusion, including at least 5 million children. But our goal is also to set a clear path to help eradicate poverty by 2050, as was committed by President von der Leyen in the State of the Union speech. Time and time again, this Parliament has reiterated its commitment to this goal and we thank you for that. We also look forward to reading the own-initiative report that you will present on the anti-poverty strategy.

Fighting poverty is not only the right thing to do, it is the smart thing to do. It builds trust in our institutions, it builds bonds in our societies, it makes us collectively more resilient and, I dare say, more secure. It supports our economic growth, bringing more people into the labour market, delivering stronger household incomes and offering, in the end, new and more opportunities for all of us. Our anti-poverty strategy will focus on what matters most: access to essential services and protections; tackling the roots of exclusion; breaking the cycle of poverty from one generation to the other; and, of course, delivering on a stronger European Child Guarantee, which will be, I repeat this, central to our efforts.

In the past months, we have also listened, to local and national leaders, to social partners, to NGOs, and most importantly, we have listened to people who know poverty because they experience it. All these voices must be involved in preparing and delivering this tool, our new strategy. We want to look at this from all angles possible because poverty is multidimensional. It is not just about salaries or work, it is not just about money. It is about access to schools, to housing; it is about energy bills; it is about food. It is also about access to key services like healthcare or like childcare. So every policy must serve inclusion.

Now, beyond Europe, in two weeks' time, the first Leaders' Meeting of the Global Alliance Against Hunger and Poverty, as well as the Second World Summit for Social Development, after 30 years, will take place in Doha. The summit will focus on fighting poverty, on creating jobs and on promoting social justice. It is a key opportunity to place the eradication of poverty at the heart of the global agenda, with a strong commitment on the part of the European Union.

Poverty, I think we can all understand that, is not destiny. It is not inevitable. It is the result of policy choices that we make. And together we can choose differently and better. I am happy to see that we share a common goal that is one to put Europe on a sustainable path of poverty reduction and over time, help eradicate poverty on our continent.

Â Â Dimitris Tsiodras, rapporteur. â Mr President, Commissioner, dear colleagues, the 'one substance, one assessment' package comprising three legislative reports is not just a technical update. It is a transformational reform in the way Europe assesses the safety of chemicals. These three laws together form the backbone of a simpler, faster and more coherent regulatory system, providing stronger protection for citizens and the environment, predictability for businesses, and legal certainty for regulators. A reform that accelerates action, enhances transparency, reinforces scientific excellence and strengthens Europe's competitiveness and innovation. We, in this House, worked in a constructive spirit to strike the right balance, simplifying procedures and reducing the administrative burden without compromising on scientific rigour or the high level of health and environmental protection that defines our Union.

A cornerstone of this achievement is the creation of the common data platform. For the first time, data from EU and national sources will be integrated into a single system, improving transparency, accessibility and efficiency. This platform will eliminate duplication, accelerate assessments and enable better, faster decisions. It will also promote the use of existing data, reducing costs and the administrative burden while helping to phase out animal testing â a long-standing ethical and scientific goal that we all share.

At the same time, we have ensured the protection of intellectual property and commercial sensitive data. Transparency must follow the principle of being as open as possible, as closed as necessary. Businesses â especially SMEs â need the confidence that their investments in research and development are secure and that fair competition is preserved. This balanced approach makes the common data platform not just a technical tool, but a strategic instrument.

Equally important, the OSOA package strengthens inter-agency cooperation between the European Chemicals Agency, the European Food Safety Authority, the European Medicines Agency and the European Environmental Agency. It clarifies roles, avoids overlaps and streamlines scientific assessments. Each agency will work within its core mandate in a spirit of complementarity and transparency.

The third equally vital pillar of this package â the directive on the European Chemicals Agency â consolidates this vision. It strengthens ECHA's role as the cornerstone of Europe's chemicals management system. We all recognise that ECHA must operate within a robust, fit-for-purpose legal framework. During the negotiations, we also took into account the operational realities of such a reform. We are extending the transitional period from 12 to 20 months to ensure ECHA can adapt, reorganise its scientific committees and maintain the quality of its assessment. In addition, we introduced a safeguard that the Commission will monitor ECHA's workload governance and resources and, if necessary, propose legislative adjustments.

Dear colleagues, the OSOA package strengthens protections for citizens and the environment and enhances the transparency and coherence of scientific assessments. It provides the legal certainty that industry needs to invest and innovate in Europe. It is proof that environmental ambition and competitiveness can go hand in hand. On behalf of the European Parliament, I am proud of what we have achieved together through constructive dialogue and compromise. So I strongly encourage you to endorse this package. Thank you.

Â Â Maria LuÃ­s Albuquerque, Member of the Commission. â Mr President, honourable Members, I am delighted to be here today as we move towards the very final steps of the legislative process on the 'one substance, one assessment' package. This package, consisting of three legislative proposals, is a clear contribution to our simplification agenda. It consolidates scientific and technical work on chemicals in the EU agencies. It also strengthens cooperation between agencies and enables easy access to data on chemicals for everyone.

Building on the 'one substance, one assessment' approach, it will improve the efficiency and coherence of safety assessments on chemicals. This package will help simplify processes, and offer greater predictability for authorities and stakeholders. Most importantly, it will help protect citizens and the environment from hazardous chemicals.

I welcome the strong interest that Parliament has shown in this legislative package, and I want to thank everyone involved for the excellent collaboration, starting with the rapporteur, honourable Member Tsiodras and all the shadow rapporteurs. With your constructive approach, we managed to reach an agreement in one single trilogue on 12Â June. I now look forward to a constructive debate today and to bringing this legislative package across the finish line together. And together, we can deliver more efficient, consistent and transparent chemical assessments to the benefit of all.

16. The decision to impose a fine on Google: defending press and media freedom in the EU (debate)
Video of the speeches
Â 

Â Â PriekÅ¡sÄdÄtÄjs. â NÄkamais darba kÄrtÄ«bas punkts ir debates par Komisijas paziÅojumu par lÄmumu uzlikt naudas sodu uzÅÄmumam âGoogleâ, aizstÄvot preses un mediju brÄ«vÄ«bu Eiropas SavienÄ«bÄ (2025/2909(RSP)).

Â 
Â Â 
Â 

Â Â Teresa Ribera, Executive Vice-President of the Commission. â Mr President, honourable Members of the Chamber. It is a great pleasure to be here this afternoon with you. Thank you for putting forward this very important debate on a very relevant topic: the state of the media and press sector in the European Union. Because we know â today perhaps more than ever â that we need a strong, independent, reliable and diverse media sector to support democratic debates and help to preserve the core European values that we once took for granted.

Let me begin by underlining the importance of the EU's competition rules and of a strong enforcement in the digital sector, and also of the broader European laws that protect media independence and diversity, such as the Media Freedom Act and the Audiovisual Media Services Directive, as well as ourÂ digital rulebook, the Digital Markets Act and the Digital Services Act. Then I come into an overview of a relevant decision we took not so far ago.

On 5Â September, the Commission made public the resolution stating that we had found that Google abused its dominant position in the display advertising technology (adtech) industry for well over a decade. Google illegally favoured its own adtech services to the detriment of its competitors, advertisers and online publishers. Its behaviour resulted in higher prices for advertisers and in a smaller cut for ad publishers, such as news media organisations.

The Commission concluded that Google's practices are illegal under the EU competition rules, and issued a EURÂ 2.95 billion fine. We also asked Google to stop this illegal behaviour, and now they have to come to tell us their plans to do so, and if they fail to come up with a viable plan, we could impose an appropriate remedy. Our decision makes clear that Google needs to propose measures that eliminate its extensive conflicts of interest in the adtech industry. For the time being, our view is that structural remedy seems to be needed to ensure this.

Allow me to make a comment on the relevance of the adtech industry. Digital advertising funds much of the online free content and services we use every day. Imagine you are reading an article on a website and an ad appears â for instance, for a well-known coffee brand. What looks like a simple ad is actually the result of a complex process powered by digital advertising technologies. This case dealt with a complex chain of intermediaries that run the real-time auctions to decide which ads we see each time that we open a website such asÂ El Pais, Le Monde or whatever other media you may want to consult on its website. Publishers depend on these ads for much of their income: income that they need to produce quality journalism and to support healthy democratic debates based on sound information. But Google's behaviour, combined with its dominance in this space, has allowed it to capture an artificially large share of the revenues that advertisers pay to policies for showing their ads, so reducing the capacity of the media sector to count on these revenues. As a result, advertisers face higher prices, which they likely pass on to final customers all across the European Union. Google also locks in publishers to its own advertising tools, making it difficult for them to switch to rivals and depriving them of choice: unfair competition.Â Google's behaviour had a negative impact on all European citizens in their day-to-day use of the web, and I was pleased to see that publishers, the different media groups and other stakeholders have welcomed this decision.

The purpose of the enforcement of the antitrust laws is not imposing fines for the sake of it: it is about changing behaviours, protecting our markets, protecting our citizens. But, for the enforcement of the regulations, we sometimes need to find the illegal behaviours. Of course, we only took action in relation to the European Union, but Google's conduct has a worldwide dimension. In April, a United States federal court upheld the main claims of the Department of Justice's complaint against Google. This case closely mirrors the issues addressed in our decision. That court also recently heard arguments on the remedies Google should put in place and is expected to issue a decision in the coming months.Â There is therefore room to ensure that Google puts in place an effective remedy on both sides of the Atlantic to address its inherent conflicts of interest. This is also the message I shared to my counterpart in the United States when I met her in New York recently, and our technical teams have been in close contact as we enter in the final stage.

But this decision should not be seen in isolation. As you know, our digital rulebook, especially the Digital Markets Act and the Digital Services Act, is designed to make online advertising more transparent for advertisers, publishers and consumers, shedding light on what is often called a black box. And there is another important issue in this debate: press publishers and media organisations not only face anti-competitive behaviour in online advertising, but may also be confronting even bigger threats. I am, of course, referring to the growing use of AI-powered summaries of news and other content, often used without consent or payment and potentially in breach of copyright laws. Because things are moving very fast when it comes to AI, which of course brings enormous opportunities and potential but, at the same time, real risk â and the threat to the media sector is one of them.

In parallel, we have just launched the evaluation of the 2009 Broadcasting Communication. And let me also mention that, last year, the co-legislators adopted the European Media Freedom Act, which sets out measures to safeguard media independence and pluralism in the internal market. It includes, in alignment with the safeguards laid down in the Digital Services Act, protections against the unwarranted removal of legitimate media content by very large online platforms, as well as new transparency requirements for advertising and audience measurement. Moreover, the Commission is evaluating the Audiovisual Media Services Directive to check if it is still meeting the needs of today's evolving market and technological realities.

Looking ahead, our upcoming European Democracy Shield will outline measures to strengthen the sustainability of the media sectors across the European Union and combat practices that can undermine democratic processes and fair elections across our Union. I am looking forward to hearing your comments and, for the time being, I only have to add: thanks a lot.
